<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="c31ba3ac75a64b42b59c095ae75557f9"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="c31ba3ac75a64b42b59c095ae75557f9">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'a93ebe29-e6b3-418c-a624-d032d79e6042'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="epub-sections"><div class="epub-section"><span class="epub-state">First published: </span><span class="epub-date">30 December 2024</span></div>
      <div class="epub-section"><a class="epub-doi" aria-label="Digital Object Identifier for enImproving the Performance of Potassium Birnessite Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries by Partial Ion Exchange link" href="https://doi.org/10.1002/bte2.20240065">https://doi.org/10.1002/bte2.20240065</a></div></div><span class="openUrl"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=s0&amp;dbid=16384&amp;doi=10.1002/bte2.20240065&amp;type=tocOpenUrl&amp;url=http%3A%2F%2Fimp-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com%2Fopenurl%2F44IMP%2FICL_VU1%3F" title="OpenURL Imperial College London" class="sfxLink visitable" target="_blank">Find at Imperial</a></span>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d191249369" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">ABSTRACT</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The current study explores the synthesis and electrochemical performance of potassium birnessite as a cathode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), achieved through partial ion exchange resulting from partial potassium deintercalation followed by sodium intercalation during the first electrochemical cycle. Three samples of potassium birnessite (KB400, KB500, and KB600) are synthesized using a sol–gel method and subsequently calcined at different temperatures to evaluate the influence of crystal water and K<sup>+</sup> ions on structural stability and their electrochemical performance. X-ray diffraction analysis confirms the formation of samples with high crystallinity. Additionally, X-ray fluorescence, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy, and thermogravimetric analysis are employed to verify their chemical composition and oxidation states. Among the samples, KB500 exhibits the most favorable electrochemical performance, achieving a specific capacity of 175 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at C/10 when cycled within a voltage range of 1.6–4.2 V. Long-term cycling tests at a narrower potential range of 2–3.6 V demonstrate promising values of 110 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in capacity for KB500, with a retention of 90% over 80 cycles. The presence of potassium and interlayer water is crucial for enhancing structural stability and ion diffusion. These findings suggest that KB500 could serve as a promising cathode material for SIBs, providing a structurally stable option for energy storage applications.</p></div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="bte220240065-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="bte220240065-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The storage of intermittently generated renewable energy continues to present challenges in meeting current demand. Despite significant advancements in storage technologies, much work remains. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are being explored as alternatives to lithium-ion batteries, but they require innovative electrode materials to improve storage capacity while minimizing environmental impact [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0001_R_d191249360e276" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chemical Reviews">1</a></span>].</p>
         
         <p>One potential cathode material is birnessite-type compounds. Jones and Milne first described birnessite in 1956 as a natural mineral found in Birness, Scotland [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0002_R_d191249360e282" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Mineralogical Magazine and Journal of the Mineralogical Society">2</a></span>]. It has garnered interest due to being the most abundant manganese-containing mineral in the Earth's crust. Birnessite features a layered structure primarily composed of manganese oxide (phyllomanganate) with edge-sharing MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra, having a nominal formula of A<sub><i>x</i></sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>·<i>n</i>H<sub>2</sub>O, where the interlayer distance is typically around 7 Å [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0003_R_d191249360e297" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chemistry of Materials">3</a></span>]. This structure results in a two-dimensional framework with some vacancies. The variable amounts of Mn<sup>4+</sup> ions are either lacking or substituted predominantly by Mn<sup>3+</sup> and secondarily by Mn<sup>2+</sup> [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0004_R_d191249360e306" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - American Mineralogist">4</a></span>]. To balance the negative charge from Mn<sup>4+</sup> vacancies and the presence of different manganese valences, birnessite incorporates cations from other elements, designated as “A” (usually alkaline and alkaline-earth metals, where <i>x</i> &lt; 0.7), located between the layers, along with “<i>n</i>” water molecules, which can move in and out of the structure [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0005_R_d191249360e316" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Journal of Materials Chemistry C">5</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0006_R_d191249360e316" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - American Mineralogist">6</a></span>].</p>
         
         <p>Birnessite can participate in cation-exchange and oxidation–reduction reactions [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0007_R_d191249360e322" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Clays and Clay Minerals">7</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0008_R_d191249360e322" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Clays and Clay Minerals">8</a></span>]. Its electrochemical behavior is largely determined by the redox activity of manganese. Layered structures with manganese as redox centers have the advantage of relatively high redox potentials. However, they also face challenges, such as lattice transformations caused by the Jahn–Teller effect and the potential dissolution of Mn<sup>2+</sup> due to the disproportionation of Mn<sup>3+</sup> into Mn<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup> during electrochemical cycling [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0009_R_d191249360e334" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces">9</a>-<a href="#bte220240065-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0011_R_d191249360e334" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Progress in Solid State Chemistry">11</a></span>].</p>
         
         <p>To address the Jahn–Teller effect and enhance stability, other transition metals like Co or Ni have been used in layered oxides; however, their use raises environmental and sustainability concerns [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0012_R_d191249360e340" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Journal of Alloys and Compounds">12</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0013_R_d191249360e340" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces">13</a></span>]. Multielement doping can yield positive results but typically requires a complex methodology [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0014_R_d191249360e343" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Battery Energy">14</a></span>]. Therefore, a more systemic strategy to resolve the issues with the pristine crystal structure, without relying on doping with other transition metals, is preferred [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0015_R_d191249360e346" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nanoscale Horizons">15</a>-<a href="#bte220240065-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0018_R_d191249360e346" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon Energy">18</a></span>]. One proposed strategy involves inducing a beneficial structural transformation by incorporating elements that convert the arrangement from a tunnel structure to a layered configuration, which shows promising potential [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0019_R_d191249360e349" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Advanced Energy Materials">19</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0020_R_d191249360e349" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Advanced Materials">20</a></span>].</p>
         
         <p>Prior studies have utilized sodium birnessite as electrode material in SIBs [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0021_R_d191249360e356" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nature Communications">21</a>-<a href="#bte220240065-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0024_R_d191249360e356" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chemistry of Materials">24</a></span>], achieving encouraging results compared to the more traditional O3-type and P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>-layered oxide counterpart [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0001_R_d191249360e364" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chemical Reviews">1</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0025_R_d191249360e364" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Science and Technology of Advanced Materials">25</a></span>]. It has been suggested that the crystal water in birnessite acts as an interlayer pillar, thus stabilizing the structure by avoiding the phase transformations observed in P2- and O3-type layered oxides. It has been suggested that the water molecules in birnessite act like interlayer pillars, stabilizing the structure and preventing phase transformations typically seen in P2- and O3-type layered oxides. Furthermore, the presence of crystal water significantly helps control undesirable Mn<sup>2+</sup> dissolution by limiting its migration within the structure. Consequently, crystal water can effectively enhance structural stability during cycling in layered materials [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0024_R_d191249360e370" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chemistry of Materials">24</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0026_R_d191249360e370" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Journal of Materials Chemistry">26</a></span>].</p>
         
         <p>Research has also assessed the impact of altering the alkali ion within the interlayer, concluding that potassium in the sodium layers can serve as a pillar to improve electrochemical performance [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0027_R_d191249360e376" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nature Communications">27</a></span>]. However, in potassium birnessite (K<sub><i>x</i></sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>·<i>y</i>H<sub>2</sub>O, where <i>x</i> &lt; 1; henceforth referred to as KB), the low K<sup>+</sup>-ion content can negatively impact its electrochemical performance as a potassium host material, leading to significant irreversible structural changes [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0028_R_d191249360e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Energy Letters">28</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0029_R_d191249360e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Energy Letters">29</a></span>]. In contrast, the empty spaces in the lattice could accommodate additional sodium ions its low K<sup>+</sup>-ion content can have a detrimental impact on its electrochemical performance as a potassium host material, resulting in severe irreversible structural changes [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0028_R_d191249360e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Energy Letters">28</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0029_R_d191249360e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Energy Letters">29</a></span>]. Nonetheless, the empty spaces in the lattice could accommodate additional sodium ions [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0030_R_d191249360e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Journal of the Electrochemical Society">30</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0031_R_d191249360e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Advanced Materials">31</a></span>].</p>
         
         <p>Thus, employing potassium birnessite as a potential SIB cathode represents a strategy to introduce mobile sodium ions while maintaining the layered structure, influenced by residual potassium and crystal water. Herein, to explore this approach, a straightforward permanganate reduction method is used to examine three different potassium birnessite samples synthesized at selected temperatures.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="bte220240065-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="bte220240065-sec-0020-title">2 Experimental Section and Methods</h2>
         
         <p>Three samples of potassium birnessite were synthesized using a modification of a previously reported sol–gel method [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0032_R_d191249360e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Inorganic Chemistry">32</a></span>]. For each synthesis, 50 mL of a 0.4 mol L<sup>–1</sup> KMnO<sub>4</sub> solution was quickly added to a flask containing 50 mL of a 25 v% ethanol solution. The mixture was vigorously stirred for 1 min and then left under agitation overnight at 300 rpm. The resulting gel was then subjected to centrifugation, after which the recovered solid was calcined at 400°C (referred to as KB400), 500°C (referred to as KB500), or 600°C (referred to as KB600) for 2 h. The calcined material was then milled into a fine dark brown powder. Subsequently, the products were thoroughly washed four times with distilled water and dried overnight at 110°C. All reagent-grade chemicals were obtained commercially from Aldrich and were used as received.</p>
         
         <p>Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was performed in air on a Shimadzu instrument, by increasing the temperature from 30 to 600°C at a rate of 5°C min<sup>−1</sup>. The powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) technique facilitated the analysis of sample crystallinity, purity, and phase composition. XRD patterns were obtained at 0.02° s<sup>–1</sup> between 5° and 90° (2θ degree) on a Bruker D8 Discover A25 diffractometer equipped with Cu K radiation and a graphite monochromator. The analysis of these results was carried out using Topas software. X-ray fluorescence (XRF) spectrometry technique (ZSX Primus IV de Rigaku) was employed to compare the actual and nominal sample compositions.</p>
         
         <p>The X-ray photoelectron emission spectroscopy (XPS) technique helped interpret the chemical state of elements in the samples. After being outgassed at a high vacuum overnight, the samples were analyzed in a SPECS Phobios 150 MCD XPS spectrometer, equipped with an Al Kα source. The C1s line of the adventitious carbon (284.6 eV) was used to calibrate binding energy positions. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images and energy-dispersive X-ray (EDX) maps were acquired in a JSM-7800F Prime JEOL Microscope. Field-emission, high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) was conducted with a TALOS F200i. <i>CrystBox</i> software was utilized to index the Fourier Transforms of the high-resolution images.</p>
         
         <p>Electrodes were prepared by mixing the active material (70 wt.%), carbon black (20 wt.%), and PVDF (polyvinylidene fluoride) (10 wt.%) in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (Emplura, 99.5%) to form a homogenous paste which was coated with an aluminum current collector. Afterward, electrodes were vacuum-dried at 120°C for 2 h. The final load of active material in the working electrode was ca. 3 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>SIBs were assembled using Swagelok-type cells in an MBraun glove box under an argon atmosphere and controlled O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O traces. A metallic sodium disk (Panreac, 99.8%) was used as the counter electrode. A solution of NaClO<sub>4</sub> (Strem, &gt; 99%) 1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> in propylene carbonate (Sigma-Aldrich, 99.7%) containing 2 wt.% of fluoroethylene carbonate (Sigma-Aldrich, 99%) (PC + 2%FEC) was employed as a solvent. Glass fiber disks (GF/A Whatman) were soaked in this solution and acted as separators between electrodes.</p>
         
         <p>Voltammetric and galvanostatic tests were conducted to characterize the materials electrochemically. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was completed in the 1.6–4.2 V potential window with scan rates between 0.1 and 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Galvanostatic cycling was carried out at different current densities from C/10 to 2 C between 1.6 and 4.2 V and 2 and 3.6 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) of pristine and cycled electrodes was performed between 1 mHz and 1 MHz, and its results were fitted to the applicable equivalent circuit.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="bte220240065-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="bte220240065-sec-0030-title">3 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The three synthesized samples, KB400, KB500, and KB600, were analyzed using TGA, XRF, and XPS to determine their chemical formulas [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0033_R_d191249360e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Chemistry of Materials">33</a></span>]. The XRF analysis resulted in an atomic ratio of <i>y</i> = K/Mn as shown in Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#bte220240065-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a> for all samples. The average oxidation state of manganese indicated as 2<i>x</i> in Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#bte220240065-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>, was determined by analyzing the deconvolution of X-ray photoelectron spectra from the pristine samples discussed below (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220240065-fig-0008">8</a>) and assessing the contributions of their relative areas. Additionally, the data from the TGA in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220240065-fig-0001">1</a> were utilized to assess the structural water content.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="bte220240065-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6094dcbb-c941-4fe2-9f54-fd14805beb6d/bte220240065-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6094dcbb-c941-4fe2-9f54-fd14805beb6d/bte220240065-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3541af29-edb3-4b6b-b3f9-06f3509d0915/bte220240065-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6094dcbb-c941-4fe2-9f54-fd14805beb6d/bte220240065-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=bte220240065-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbte2.20240065" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TGA curves of synthesized KB samples.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220240065-fig-0001">1</a>, the TGA plots indicate a minor weight loss occurring below 120°C, which is attributed to the release of adsorbed water. All samples exhibited a significant weight loss step around 150°C, associated with the loss of interlayer crystal water. As shown in Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#bte220240065-tbl-0001">1</a>, this weight loss value decreased as the calcination temperature increased. Finally, the stabilization of the weight loss observed after approximately 390°C can be attributed to the final step of dihydroxylation of the layer's hydroxyl groups, resulting in a water loss of about 0.5 wt.%.</p>

         <p>Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#bte220240065-tbl-0001">1</a> summarizes the previous results and corresponding values for each KB sample.</p>
         
         <p>In all KB samples, the potassium content was approximately 0.23 atoms per unit formula. Furthermore, the average oxidation state of manganese was around 3.9+, with about 10% of the manganese present as Mn<sup>3+</sup>. This combination theoretically prevents the occurrence of Jahn–Teller distortion [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0034_R_d191249360e810" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Chemistry of Materials">34</a></span>].</p>
         
         <p>XRD analysis revealed a series of narrow reflections in the XRD patterns, indicating the presence of highly crystalline materials, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220240065-fig-0002">2</a>. The indexation of these patterns through Rietveld analysis identified two phases in all samples: birnessite (<i>C12/m1</i>) and hausmannite (<i>I4</i><sub><i>1</i></sub><i>/amd</i>). Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#bte220240065-tbl-0002">2</a> presents the refined crystallographic parameters along with their corresponding phase percentages.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="bte220240065-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2228a23a-20f9-4e60-a628-d8079cfafc96/bte220240065-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2228a23a-20f9-4e60-a628-d8079cfafc96/bte220240065-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/63aa28a2-4b6b-48c6-b19a-0515dd58a7b9/bte220240065-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2228a23a-20f9-4e60-a628-d8079cfafc96/bte220240065-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

         </section>

            <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
         </div>
         <p>Several studies on the formation of pure KB have identified various synthesis factors that need to be considered [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0035_R_d191249360e1105" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Journal of Solid State Chemistry">35</a>-<a href="#bte220240065-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0037_R_d191249360e1105" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Clays and Clay Minerals">37</a></span>]. However, even when these parameters are controlled, the initial stages of the reaction lead to the spontaneous formation of manganese (II) and (III) hydroxides, which have low solubility [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0038_R_d191249360e1108" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Journal of the Electrochemical Society">38</a></span>]. At this stage, hausmannite is likely to present, as it is thermodynamically favored and cannot convert into birnessite.</p>
         
         <p>Additionally, the calcination temperature significantly affects the release of crystal water, as indicated by TGA. This process facilitates the formation of the dehydrated hausmannite phase. As a result, all samples analyzed through the Rietveld method showed the presence of hausmannite [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0039_R_d191249360e1114" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Clays and Clay Minerals">39</a></span>].</p>
         
         <p>The purity of birnessite in all samples exceeded 97%, indicated by the presence of two main diffraction peaks at 12.5° and 25°. These peaks correspond to the layer-to-layer distance (<i>d</i><sub>001</sub> = 7.08 Å) and the layer-to-water interlayer distance (<i>d</i><sub>002</sub> = 3.54 Å), respectively. No significant changes in the cell parameters of the three samples were observed. Upon closer examination, a slight reduction in cell volume was inferred from the minor displacement of the main reflections to higher angles as the temperature increased. This small decrease may be attributed to the loss of crystal water within the formula unit, as noted in Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#bte220240065-tbl-0001">1</a>. Additionally, a slight reduction in the relative intensity of the (002) reflection was observed in the samples that were calcined at higher temperatures.</p>
         
         <p>CV was conducted on the samples. The resulting curves, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220240065-fig-0003">3</a>, displayed the most intense anodic and cathodic peaks at approximately 2.67 V/2.13 V and 3.54 V/2.94 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. During the intercalation and deintercalation processes of sodium ions, the large size of these ions can create significant strain on the surrounding MnO<sub>6</sub> layers, subsequently modifying the coordination environment of manganese. Consequently, several redox couples have been previously associated with the structural changes that occur during the insertion and extraction of alkali ions [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0040_R_d191249360e1139" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Advanced Energy Materials">40</a>-<a href="#bte220240065-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0043_R_d191249360e1139" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Advanced Energy Materials">43</a></span>]. Nevertheless, the main activity of all these peaks could be linked up only to the activity of Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup>, as observed in the XPS results, as discussed below.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="bte220240065-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7b54321c-27bc-4d25-8498-d5abb6321564/bte220240065-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7b54321c-27bc-4d25-8498-d5abb6321564/bte220240065-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3decc1fd-4efb-4f1c-b3ed-3bb5d2471d83/bte220240065-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7b54321c-27bc-4d25-8498-d5abb6321564/bte220240065-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=bte220240065-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbte2.20240065" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Cyclic voltammetry (CV) of all three samples KB400, KB500, and KB600, performed at sweep rate <i>ν</i>: 0.1, 0.3, 0.5, 0.7, and 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Two C-rate performance tests were conducted on the samples over a wide potential range (WR: 1.6–4.2 V vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and a narrow range (NR: 2–3.6 V vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). These tests encompassed the full range of electrochemical activity previously observed in the CV measurements. The first cycle began charging from the open-circuit voltage (OCV) after the assembly was completed, reaching the maximum voltage for each range. The results of these tests are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220240065-fig-0004">4</a>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="bte220240065-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7cb1de46-523c-400f-9f5b-95dec5b885fe/bte220240065-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7cb1de46-523c-400f-9f5b-95dec5b885fe/bte220240065-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/93297ccb-ed05-4fc0-952d-132d392afd53/bte220240065-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7cb1de46-523c-400f-9f5b-95dec5b885fe/bte220240065-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=bte220240065-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbte2.20240065" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">C-rate at two potential ranges: Wide range (WR: 1.6–4.2 V vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and narrow range (NR: 2.0–3.6 V vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), for all three samples.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In both potential ranges, the highest capacity values were observed for KB500 and KB400 at low C/10 kinetics (wide range [WR]: 175 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, narrow range [NR]: 105 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), while the capacity value of KB600 was 30% lower. Although KB600 demonstrated a 99% capacity retention across each cycling rate, at the highest evaluated kinetics of 2 C, the KB500 sample showed remarkable performance with high values such as ca. 100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in WR and ca. 55 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in NR.</p>
         
         <p>Interestingly, when returning to the 1 C rate, the KB500 demonstrated a 12% improvement in performance recovery (WR: 123 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, NR: 76 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) compared to the first round (WR: 105 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, NR: 68 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). When returning to C/10, the trend in capacity for long cycling was maintained in all three samples. This study concluded that the best-performing sample in terms of capacity at low kinetics was KB500, with good performance related to capacity retention along cycling kinetics and enhanced recovery when returning to the lowest kinetics after C-rate.</p>
         
         <p>Prolonged cycling at C/10 was also performed within the narrow voltage range of 2.0–3.6 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na as Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220240065-fig-0005">5</a> summarizes. The charge and discharge profiles for Cycles 1 and 2 of the sample KB500 are shown in the inset as an example for further discussion.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="bte220240065-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e631f388-3e81-456b-adc2-a099d2c3805f/bte220240065-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e631f388-3e81-456b-adc2-a099d2c3805f/bte220240065-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b49962ba-8026-4e99-b511-013d16445647/bte220240065-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e631f388-3e81-456b-adc2-a099d2c3805f/bte220240065-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=bte220240065-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbte2.20240065" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Long cycling at C/10 for all samples in NR (2.0–3.6 V vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). Inset: Charge and discharge profile for Cycle 1 (starting in charge) and Cycle 2 for KB500 sample.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>During the cycling process, it was expected that complete extraction of K ions would occur during the initial cell charge. Nonetheless, the initial charge capacity of the samples was ca. 5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (as shown in the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220240065-fig-0005">5</a>), which represents only about 8% of the theoretical initial charging capacity for the synthesized KBs (65 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). In contrast, the initial charge of sample KB500 cycled within a wide potential range reached around 50 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (as shown in the inset of Supporting Information S1: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>), accounting for 80% of the theoretical initial charging capacity. The second charge displayed a higher capacity, which can be attributed to the partial substitution of potassium ions by sodium ions due to the latter's smaller ionic radius. Once the first discharge occurs, sodium ions from the electrolyte enter the structure through electrochemical intercalation, prompted by the potential difference. At this stage, ion exchange can take place. Na<sup>+</sup> ions can be extracted during the second charge, resulting in more vacancies for the subsequent discharge and enhancing the cycling efficiency.</p>
         
         <p>Based on these results, the incomplete K<sup>+</sup>-ions extraction can be inferred. Therefore, it is speculated that the remaining potassium within the structure might play a crucial role in maintaining the structural integrity necessary for stability during cycling [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0027_R_d191249360e1285" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nature Communications">27</a></span>], and the subsequent discharge of the cell could be associated with sodium intercalation.</p>
         
         <p>The presence of interlayer crystal water mitigates the ionic size disparity in the alkali metal layer, thereby preventing the collapse of the structure after the ionic exchange of sodium and potassium [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0004_R_d191249360e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - American Mineralogist">4</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0005_R_d191249360e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Journal of Materials Chemistry C">5</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0022_R_d191249360e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - CrystEngComm">22</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0044_R_d191249360e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Chemistry of Materials">44</a></span>].</p>
         
         <p>Besides, the electrochemical response of the samples showed some remarkable aspects. KB400 and KB500 samples exhibited initial capacity values of ca. 105 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, while KB600 barely reached 75 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Despite capacity reduction due to the narrowing of the potential window, these values respectively correspond to 46% and 33% of the theoretical capacity (227 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) according to the complete redox activity of the Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> redox couple for all available manganese. On the other hand, KB400 exhibited a capacity drop of 30% after 80 cycles. Despite its lower initial value, the KB600 sample only had an initial loss capacity of 15% after 80 cycles. In addition, the sample KB500 exhibited good retention in the capacity of ca. 90% after 80 cycles. The difference between theoretical and actual capacity suggests the presence of remaining K<sup>+</sup> ions in the structure. The operation in the NR represents a means of working with competitive results to maintain the integrity of the material structure. This approach allows excellent reversibility of sodium intercalation [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0027_R_d191249360e1310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nature Communications">27</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0045_R_d191249360e1310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces">45</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0046_R_d191249360e1310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Physical Review Letters">46</a></span>].</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Furthermore, the apparent sodium diffusion coefficients (D<sub>Na+</sub>) were determined from the CV curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220240065-fig-0003">3</a>) considering the linear dependence on the current intensity (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) of the most intense anodic/cathodic peaks at 2.67/2.13 and 3.54/2.94 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, and the square root of the sweep rate, <i>ν</i><sup>1/2</sup>, as described in Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#bte220240065-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>:

                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>where <i>“n</i>” is the number of transferred electrons (in this case, <i>n</i> = 1), <i>“S”</i> is the geometric electrode area, and “<i>C</i>” is the Na<sup>+</sup>-ions concentration in the electrolyte. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220240065-fig-0006">6</a> shows the good fit of both samples with the linearity of the Randles–Sevčík equation [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0047_R_d191249360e1392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Transactions of the Faraday Society">47</a></span>] (Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#bte220240065-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>).</div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="bte220240065-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/26e89b9c-f232-4216-b3e7-8e8de51ccaed/bte220240065-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/26e89b9c-f232-4216-b3e7-8e8de51ccaed/bte220240065-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f011286f-3c8e-4967-9a82-e79a9b12bfdd/bte220240065-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/26e89b9c-f232-4216-b3e7-8e8de51ccaed/bte220240065-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=bte220240065-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbte2.20240065" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Relation between the peak current (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) and the square root of the sweep rate (ν<sup>1/2</sup>) of the anodic peaks (1: at ca. 2.7 V and 2: ca. 3.5 V) and cathodic peaks (3: at ca. 2.9 V and 4: at ca. 2.1 V).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The apparent diffusion coefficients (D<sub>Na+</sub>) for each of the four peaks are shown in Supporting Information S1: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>. The average obtained values were slightly higher for sample KB500 (ca. 1.4 × 10<sup>–12 </sup>cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>) than those for sample KB400 (ca. 1.1 × 10<sup>–12 </sup>cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>); meanwhile, KB600 had average values with a lower magnitude order (ca. 9.6 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>).</p>
         
         <p>Herein, it should be noted that while an increase in lattice spacing has been observed to enhance ionic diffusion kinetics, other factors must also be considered. KB400 had a slightly larger interlayer space than KB500. However, it has a slightly lower diffusion coefficient. It has been observed that the amount of water of crystallization present has a synergistic influence on the process by affecting electrostatic charge screening, alongside the increase of the interlayer distance [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0024_R_d191249360e1455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chemistry of Materials">24</a></span>]. For this reason, sample KB500 was chosen for further detailed characterization. Supporting Information S1: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> shows the rate capability charge and discharge profiles for KB500 sample in both potential windows—WR and NR—obtained from the experiments in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220240065-fig-0004">4</a>.</p>
         
         <p>Besides, the results of ex situ XRD in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220240065-fig-0007">7</a> for the fully charged and discharged sample KB500 afforded a deeper interpretation of how the lattice compound changed during cycling. The broadened signals masking the main reflections corresponded to the Kapton polyimide film used to protect the electrode. In addition, reflections at 45° (2θ) in charged and discharged sample patterns corresponded to the aluminum current collector.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="bte220240065-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e502f9ed-5293-404b-a078-44bd32de61cb/bte220240065-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e502f9ed-5293-404b-a078-44bd32de61cb/bte220240065-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/66e57e6d-1478-4c9f-8a34-20e1af57699a/bte220240065-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e502f9ed-5293-404b-a078-44bd32de61cb/bte220240065-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=bte220240065-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbte2.20240065" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XRD patterns of pristine KB500 and fully charged and fully discharged.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The unit cell parameters presented in Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#bte220240065-tbl-0003">3</a> show a significant contraction in cell volume by the end of the charge branch. After the first complete cycle, a slight expansion of the unit cell was noted. However, the initial unit cell volume of the pristine compound was not reached, indicating that sodium was inserted during the initial discharge process.</p>

            <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
         </div>
         <p>The value for the interlaminar space, d<sub>001</sub>, changed significantly from 7.08 Å to 6.88 Å when the cell was fully charged. This cell contraction is consistent with the partial extraction of potassium. Conversely, after the cell was fully discharged, the interlaminar space value of d001 decreased further to 6.56 Å, suggesting that sodium ions were effectively inserted into the interlayer space. Although the theoretical value for a cell containing only sodium ions is approximately 5 Å [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0021_R_d191249360e1632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nature Communications">21</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0024_R_d191249360e1632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chemistry of Materials">24</a></span>], the intermediate value of 6.56 Å supports the hypothesis that potassium ions were still present in the cell during the insertion of sodium ions.</p>
         
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220240065-fig-0008">8</a> illustrates the Mn2p, O1s, and K2p regions of the XPS spectra for the raw sample. In the Mn2p region, the asymmetric profiles indicate the presence of two components: Mn<sup>3+</sup> at 642 eV and 653.6 eV and Mn<sup>4+</sup> at 644 eV and 655 eV, corresponding to the Mn2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Mn2p<sub>1/2</sub> core levels, respectively [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0048_R_d191249360e1651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Electrochimica Acta">48</a></span>]. Satellite peaks are also observed. Following full charge, the signals associated with tetravalent manganese increase due to oxidation. Upon further discharge, a small contribution appears at lower binding energy, which may be attributed to the presence of Mn<sup>2+</sup> (640.5 and 652.4 eV).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="bte220240065-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6a0d91a9-ae83-42bd-b2f4-01a7877de784/bte220240065-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6a0d91a9-ae83-42bd-b2f4-01a7877de784/bte220240065-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3bdef015-ddf5-4cc1-963e-18d970047326/bte220240065-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6a0d91a9-ae83-42bd-b2f4-01a7877de784/bte220240065-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=bte220240065-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbte2.20240065" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS regions of KB500 sample: raw, fully charged, and fully discharged.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The O1s region acquired for the raw sample shows an asymmetric profile that can be decomposed into three components at 530 eV ascribable to the oxide atoms in the lattice, as well as two additional lines 531.5 and 532.6 eV coming from the surface oxide and carbonate species [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0049_R_d191249360e1679" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - ChemElectroChem">49</a></span>]. During charging, the latter contribution increased irreversibly due to the formation of a solid–electrolyte interface on the electrode surface.</p>
         
         <p>Finally, the presence of potassium is clearly indicated by the K2p<sub>1/2</sub> and K2p<sub>3/2</sub> bands. On the other hand, there is a significant reduction in the intensity of the K2p signals after oxidation, suggesting that potassium is only partially extracted from the network during cell charging. Additionally, these bands shift to higher binding energies, which indicates the formation of a more ionic bond between potassium and its surrounding environment. It is also possible that K–F bonds form due to the reaction of potassium with FEC or PVDF [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0050_R_d191249360e1689" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces">50</a></span>].</p>
         
         <p>SEM images of the pristine KB500 electrode (Supporting Information S1: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a</a>) revealed an elongated morphology resembling fibers or needles of varying submicron dimensions. These structures form a dense, porous three-dimensional network at the microscopic level, like that of a sea urchin. Additionally, the EDX elemental surface mapping derived from the SEM images demonstrated a uniform distribution of elements across the surface. In contrast, the SEM images of the cycled material at the end of the 80th discharge (Supporting Information S1: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4b</a>) showed the formation of clusters of needles, which exhibited a slight flattening of the overall morphology. Furthermore, the EDS mapping conducted on this cycled sample did not indicate the presence of potassium in the electrode, suggesting effective ion exchange. It is possible that K<sup>+</sup> ions were transferred to the separator material, as has been reported by other authors.</p>
         
         <p>On the other hand, the HRTEM images of sample KB500 (Supporting Information S1: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>) displayed individual layers with crystalline domains and lattice fringes, as well as Fourier Transform characteristics of the &lt; 010 &gt; zone axis (a) and basal (001) planes (b).</p>
         
         <p>To examine how the resistance at the electrode–electrolyte interface affects the overall cell performance, EIS was conducted after the 1st, 4th, and 100th cycles at a rate of C/10. The Nyquist plots for samples KB500 are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#bte220240065-fig-0009">9</a>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="bte220240065-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/486e20b8-71d8-4961-a62a-2373dee4fa9b/bte220240065-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/486e20b8-71d8-4961-a62a-2373dee4fa9b/bte220240065-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/49afea48-015d-48fa-9bba-9e39103672b8/bte220240065-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/486e20b8-71d8-4961-a62a-2373dee4fa9b/bte220240065-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 9<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=bte220240065-fig-0009&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbte2.20240065" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Impedance spectra of selected samples. The equivalent circuit used in the fitting is shown as insets. The fitting curves are shown as continuous lines.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Their recorded spectra were found to be complex, thereby reflecting multiple time constants related to bulk and interfacial transport processes. Nonetheless, the data revealed a distinct semicircle in the high-frequency range, followed by another semicircle at moderate frequencies that merged into an oblique line in the low-frequency range. This latter plot can be attributed to the resistance effect of the SEI, confirming its formation and evolution. However, microscopy images presented challenges in clearly identifying this effect using electronic scanning (see Supporting Information S1: Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>). The impedance spectra were fitted to the equivalent circuit shown in the inset of Figure 11, which aligns with findings from previous reports [<span><a href="#bte220240065-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0018_R_d191249360e1742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon Energy">18</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0020_R_d191249360e1742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Advanced Materials">20</a>, <a href="#bte220240065-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="bte220240065-bib-0051_R_d191249360e1742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Battery Energy">51</a></span>]. The results for internal resistances at the electrolyte (R<sub>el</sub>), the surface layer (R<sub>sl</sub>), and charge-transfer reaction (R<sub>ct</sub>) at the electrode/electrolyte interphase are shown in Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#bte220240065-tbl-0004">4</a>.</p>
         
         <div class="article-table-content" id="bte220240065-tbl-0004">
            
            
            
            
            <header class="article-table-caption"><span class="table-caption__label">Table  4.
                    </span>EIS fitting results of the selected samples.</header>
            
            
            
            <div class="article-table-content-wrapper" tabindex="0">
               <table class="table article-section__table">

                     </thead>

            </div>
            
            <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
         </div>
         <p>The slight increase in resistance observed during cycling may be attributed to the maintenance of a larger interlayer distance, which is crucial for reducing material resistance. At the same time, the interlayer space created by the remaining potassium pillars and the crystal water within the structure enhances both cycling and rate performance. This improvement helps address the challenges posed by the size of Na<sup>+</sup> ions and their impact on structural stability.</p>
         
         <p>Finally, a summary comparing sodium storage performance between KB500 and other reported cathodes is presented in Supporting Information S1: Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>. The results of this study align well with other birnessite compounds used in SIBs documented in the literature.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="bte220240065-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="bte220240065-sec-0040-title">4 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this study, three samples of potassium birnessite—identified as KB400, KB500, and KB600—were successfully synthesized using a modified sol–gel method. The samples were calcined at three different temperatures (400, 500, and 600°C) to investigate their structural and electrochemical properties as cathode materials for SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>XRD analysis confirmed the formation of highly crystalline potassium birnessite in all three samples, with a purity exceeding 97%. Additional techniques, including XRF, XPS, and TGA, elucidated the chemical composition and oxidation states of the synthesized materials. All samples exhibited a potassium-to-manganese (K/Mn) ratio of approximately 0.22. All samples had manganese with an average oxidation state of 3.9+, which reduces Jahn–Teller distortion within the framework. Furthermore, lower calcination temperatures resulted in more retained crystal water.</p>
         
         <p>Among the three samples, KB500 demonstrated the best electrochemical performance as a cathode for SIBs, achieving high-capacity values of 105 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> within a potential range of 2–3.6 V, along with a stable capacity retention of about 90% over multiple cycles. Sample KB400 showed similar initial performance but experienced more capacity fade, while KB600, despite having a lower initial capacity, exhibited better retention throughout cycling. Additionally, KB500 showcased excellent capacity retention with varying kinetics during the rate capability test, recovering effectively when returning to low current rates after exposure to elevated kinetics.</p>
         
         <p>Diffusion coefficients for sodium ions were higher for KB500 compared to KB400 and KB600, indicating that both the crystal water content and structural factors in KB500 facilitated better ionic mobility and enhanced its performance in SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>The role of interlayer water and pillaring potassium ions was critical in stabilizing the birnessite structure during cycling. Ex situ XRD of KB500 after cycling revealed a reversible contraction and expansion of the unit cell during charge and discharge processes, demonstrating the capacity of the birnessite structure to accommodate Na⁺ intercalation with minimal irreversible structural changes. The ability to maintain its crystal structure throughout cycling significantly contributed to its high capacity retention.</p>
         
         <p>Overall, KB500 has shown promise as an alternative to conventional transition-metal oxide cathodes in SIBs, due to its structural stability, enhanced sodium-ion diffusion, and long-term cycling performance. These findings lay a foundation for further research on birnessite-based materials in energy storage applications, aimed at optimizing their performance for sustainable energy storage.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
